Capital II: On fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are multiple, and their shapes are diverse, with distinct causes, for some fractures occur due to blunt force trauma that penetrates the entire bone, which is then destroyed, as in the case of a club, and this type of fracture is called ascal; or the blunt force cuts into part of the bone and also pierces the skin but does not pass through to the other side; and this type of fracture is called absolute levation.
And the wound of these two types of fractures is either large or small, and whether it contracts or expands; and its cause is due to blows with a hammer or axe on rocks, and similar instruments.
And this type of fracture is penetrating, approaching the muscle layer that is below the bone; or it is on the face of the bone, and the wound of these two types of fractures can be either large or small.
And this type of fracture is when it is narrowed within the bone in its subtlety (tiny cavities), and for this reason, this type of fracture is called capillary fracture.
The fracture that is caused by striking with a hammer or blunt force on rocks, which causes some layer of the bone to split at its joints, and occurs in concave areas such as those found in millstones when they are struck and split at their joints from within.
Furthermore, this type of fracture occurs in bones with a spongy texture, such as those found in infant skulls.
And all these types of fractures are treated as problems similar to separated stars (stars that have moved out of their proper position and require treatment). And you will find a remedy for all of it in its place.
We will recognize all types of fracture by examination on it, and through testing, we will find the substances corrupting them, which are on it.
A hair-like substance is recognized by this that is discovered on the bone, and it is scraped off, and it turns black due to the test, because the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fracture, it is first necessary to consider the condition of the patient, and then if you see by the signs that it indicates clearly by manifestation of fear, such as vomiting of bile, extension, loss of consciousness, abscess of the throat, fainting, acute fever, swelling and redness of the affected areas, and similar signs in greater dispositions, there is doubt that it will not heal.
If there are no unusual signs, you hope for health, then you begin the treatment of it.
The cause is that if it has been brought to you at the beginning as it was brought in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you study the removal of the bone on the twelfth day after all preparation.
If it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you take out the bone on the seventh day, knowing that if the bone is left, it may be corrupt from putrefaction and the signs that I have mentioned will occur.
If the fracture has reached the panniculus covering the brain and is small pieces and stuck together, then it is necessary to cut open the divided piece and separate it, as I will recommend to you.
It is that you reason with the head of the affected patient and discover on the bone according to custom and your ability according to the appearance of the wound, and it should be gentle on the patient.
If you find that there is bleeding or an abscess on the bone, then you must apply what is necessary, and it is that you put compresses of dampened wool soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess is calm, and be sure of the blood flow; and then begin with the circle around the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And the first method is that you make an incision in the bone with a narrow incision tool, and this is the form of it: Place another cutting tool next to it, slightly deeper than the previous one; and this is the form of it:
And place the cutting tool slightly deeper than the previous one.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incision tools, some of which are longer than others; and some of them are shorter than the others and are at the end of sharpness of their tips, and they may be made of iron or steel.
Use them easily for striking on the incision tool so that you do not hit the head and cut it.
If the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary to drill a hole around it before administering the incision tools with taravels, which are called non-penetrating taravels.
They are not called this only because they do not penetrate the end of the bone to the test, but rather on the taravel is rounded or curved edge around that which is left of the head of it, sharp like a margin or small circle, which prohibits submerging and passing through the thickness of the bone.
It is necessary that you take a great number of these splinters, each one suitable for the specific bone, until it is present in all parts of the brain whose extremity is sharp, and the quantity in length and width should be according to the amount of splinter needed for this bone.
This is the form of three types of splinters: The quality of perforating in the circle of the broken bone is that you place the splinter on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; and then allow the splinter to move to another location, and place what is between all around the ray or surround the surrounding area with it.
Then insert the splinters with the tweezers between each crack of the bone and make the splinter as thin as possible so that it penetrates to the end of the cavity, as mentioned before, until you pierce the bone, or do this with your hand, or by any other means using various instruments such as tongs and fine forceps.
It is necessary that you keep caution so that the tweezers do not touch any nerve.
And when you perforate the bone and it is continuous with the nerve if it is separated from it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you shave and file it on the roughness of it where it is in the bone with another tool similar to a tweezers, but nevertheless it is necessary that it be thinner and more delicate than the other tweezers.
If any small and stubborn pieces of the bones remain, then take them easily with whatever tool you have at hand; and care for the wound with linseed oil and bandages as I have said earlier.
The other method of treatment is easy and far from fear, which is called Guillaume and is highly praised; and this is the explanation of it: it is necessary for you at first to start by discovering the fracture of the bone in the location where it is most violent and manifest, until when you have discovered that part of the bone and have placed a cushion under it, the end of the cautery should be slow and gentle. Here is the shape:
The concave fracture is round and does not penetrate anything, while the acute fracture is from one side to two sides in length, as sometimes happens; that the concave fracture should be adjusted to the nerve with the face of the penetrating acute part of the bone.
Then pierce one part of the fracture with a small hammer until you have pierced the entire bone easily as it is rotated, and you should be certain that no panic occurs in any way, and if the patient was the most foolish and ignorant of all men and the most asleep. Certainly, and if he was asleep.
Then if any nerve is still attached to the bone of the panic in some places of the bone, pierce it with the end of the cautery that is concave and release it easily, because it is released from it without harm and fear.
If the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the nerve, and what has been cut off or remains of the bone is its face, and it remains rough and stubborn in places, then it is necessary that you shave that roughness and remove those stubborn areas with fine razors that you have prepared in different quantities so that it is possible for you to administer them in all places according to the most convenient places and the proper ones of them according to where you lose your operation of the broken bone and its figure.
It is necessary that you administer with the razor that you use to shave the bone at first, and place it close to the most delicate part of it, and do not stop doing this continuously until you reach the point where you can administer the thinnest and most delicate of them all.
But in each of the other small capillary and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer care according to what you have learned for the correction of it.
It is because not all those who have studied that art are capable of doing this. And it is based on what we have shown in great fractures.
And if the nerve is rectangular when you pierce the bone, then take linen according to the size of the wound, immerse it in rose oil and place it over the opening of the wound; and take another linen folded or tripled, immerse it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first linen gently but not too hard on the nerve; and administer a mild lint ointment over it and do not press it except to the extent that the wound requires.
Leave that in this care for a day or two until you are sure of the abscess forming; and then remove it and administer medications that are excessive dryness, such as linen rind and barley flour, and powdered alum and arsenic, and similar to these.
It is that you make powders of these medications that are powdered over the wound, such as dry ones; and in general, administer all the medicine of properties that absorb and do not cause pain.
Keep in mind that the wound in your treatment should be clean; and it should not be contaminated with linseed oil or any other substance; nor should you leave it to become infected; because when it is pressed against the nerve of the brain, it corrodes it and poisons it, and causes evil on the patient with malicious intent.
And when a tumor occurs at the skull's base, when the bone is modified and especially when its treatment begins, and it turns black on its surface; then consider whether the patient experiences the symptoms we mentioned, know that this is dangerous in all respects.
And if the blackness does not occur due to the medicine that is paused, but it is due to the nature of the medicine that causes the blackness, then you must take a part of honey and three parts of oil. and apply those lines on a cloth and place it over the skull; and apply care with the appropriate medicinal herbs until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not broken in the nose, but rather two fractures together, or one of them is visible, because there are two bones in it, and the fracture is cartilaginous, so it is not broken and does not occur there, but rather an attrition, a curvature, and a narrowing.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you must insert the small finger into the nose and grasp that fracture with your finger, and push it inward, until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this for your convenience, but be careful not to harm the patient in the process.
If the fracture is in the superior part of the nose, and it protrudes there, then you must align it with the end of the bone where there is a slight swelling.
If the fracture is in two parts, you must perform the same procedure.
Try to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; otherwise, apply caution for the seventh or tenth day after the abscess has healed.
Place a pad of cloth at the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or place two cotton swabs if the fracture is in two parts, and fill the cavity of the nose with enough cotton to block it.
Some of the old restorers say that cotton should be wrapped in linen and kept tied for all days, but I do not see this; rather, the cotton must be wrapped in alum-water with ground powder; and keep it there until the bone is healed and the cartilage has hardened.
In some cases, insert a piece of soft lambskin into the nose instead of cotton, as it may be difficult to place cotton because it is wrapped in cloth; and this is if the fracture of the nose is very severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing through it.
This method is not necessary for you; and if you wish to do so, perform the procedure, and if you wish to use cotton, use it.
And if another abscess occurs, fill the nose with cherry or almond oil infused in vinegar and olive oil, or with egg yolk.
But if there is no abscess, you must fill it with soda flour and ground cinnamon mixed with alum water, and place a piece of soft lambskin over it, and do not let the nose come into contact with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted fracture, then you must saw through them, extract those pieces with the appropriate instruments; and sew up the wound, and care for it with what strengthens and consolidates it, and apply the appropriate ointments.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, you must care for it with cotton and administer lead cannula until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jawbone when it is broken.
If the lower jaw is broken but the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, then consider whether the fracture is only on the outside and is not broken in two and curved towards the inside, its treatment is easy.
If the fracture is in the right part, you must insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is in the left mandible, then insert the right index finger; and push gently with that finger toward the inner side of the fracture to facilitate the separation of the broken parts from within, and with your other hand outside of the bone, you should also separate the broken pieces evenly.
If the fracture of the jaw has already been separated into two parts, then you must administer extension of both parts according to right angle until possible alignment is achieved.
If there is a loosening or disruption of teeth, you should strengthen the ones that you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or suture thread; and place cotton on the broken jaw, and double cotton, and a large sterile gauze piece properly made, or a single piece of sola equal to the length of the jaw; and bind it as appropriate according to your ability to apply the binding, until it is no longer destroyed.
Command the patient to rest and eat sweet lemons.
If you suspect that the figure has been altered in some way, then examine and remove it on the third day; and correct what has already been altered and apply it with powder of milled pearl, egg white or rice flour, as you will have removed the cotton from it, and place over the applied linen a piece of lion wool.
And then, when the plaster is attached and adjusted on it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, as it should not be removed until healed and strengthened; for most often this fracture heals in three weeks.
If another swelling appears as an abscess, administer what was mentioned earlier in its treatment until it is healed and the abscess is removed.
If the fracture is with a wound, consider if it is a separated bone or a splinter; then carefully remove those splinters with what is appropriate for removing them from the wound using instruments.
If the orifices of the wound are narrow, you should enlarge them with a flebotome according to your need.
When you have removed the splinters and there is no pus remaining, you should close the large orifices of the wound; and if it is small, place one of the dressings that is appropriate for it and apply a dressing to it until it is healed.
Capital V.
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Most often, the clavicle is broken at the midsection through the muscle, and that fracture can occur in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, separated or split into two parts without splinters, it is easier to restore; or it is such that there are splinters present and difficult to restore; or it is such that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is to have two assistants present; one should support and hold the broken clavicle while the other extends the neck on that side.
And align the fracture with your thumbs until its form is restored according to what is necessary, and neither protrude nor concave.
If additional extension is needed, then you must support the patient's head with a cloth or wool and its size according to your need; extend and raise the clavicle and hold it in place with your hand until you align the fracture as necessary.
And if you cannot extend the end of the clavicle to the outside parts, because it is blocked by a protrusion, then the patient should be reclined on his neck; and place one hand in the middle-sized size on the muscle there.
And the assistant presses the muscle on that side towards the outside until the end of the clavicle is raised above the protrusion and then you correct the fracture and align it with your thumbs.
But if you feel splinters in the clavicle and they are harmful, then it is necessary to break and remove those splinters with ease.
If it is held in the bone, then you must make an incision in it with one of the instruments that you have prepared for this purpose, as you have placed the chisel under the clavicle; and this tool has a wooden or iron handle, which takes the following form:
And is similar to the shape of a cauldron's concavity; and its width should be according to what you need for it, depending on the size of the bone or the depth of it.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for the operation; as it often has two ends, one end should be large and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound that you have incised when extracting the splinter from the bone is wide and you are sure of an abscess, then you should adjust the two lips of the wound with suture.
If the rupture is small, or you fear an abscess, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's cut.
If an abscess is feared, then you pour boiling vinegar and rose oil onto it and leave it.
And be careful that the splint does not harm the fractured bone; and for this reason, you must check the patient daily to see if their pain is lessening or if the splint needs to be removed. When the wound has healed, then you should reset the fracture and strengthen the cast.
The author advises placing the patient's head on their chest and keeping them awake for a short while to elevate the affected area and help raise the shoulders, which in turn raises the fracture of the collarbone to a higher position. Alternatively, the arms can be tied to the patient's neck.
Do not remove the dressing unless there is pus or pressure buildup within the first twelve days.
The author advises renewing the plaster if you see any signs of infection or poor healing and making any necessary adjustments to ensure proper alignment and healing of the collarbone fracture. Leave it in place until the fracture has healed completely and coagulated.
The fracture should be strengthened and supported for a period of 28 days, with some men requiring additional time to heal during this period.
Chapter VI.
On the repair of shoulder fractures.
The shoulder blade is fractured at its wide end and not at the narrow tip.
And since the location of the fracture is not visible, it must be diagnosed by touch. Therefore, prepare for the type of fracture and reduce it according to its natural shape in any way that is possible using your ingenuity.
Then place a cushion of fine powder made from milled alum and hull, stop the wound with lion's wool; then place a double-layered powder cushion; then place a cushion on the steel rod of a fine table according to the amount of the whole spatula or a little more.
But if the rod is concave at any of the places on the spatula, then you fill up that concavity with lion's wool, and adjust the rod to be level.
Then apply strong and warm liniment along with a suitable constriction and secure the rod firmly into its place so that it is not protruding from its location.
Consider the liniment daily; and whenever it is softened, apply another layer and adjust the rod if it is protruding from its location.
And be careful not to press down on the patient's wound.
The spatula is healed in 20 days or 35 days (depending on the individual's condition).
And when this period is completed, you dissolve the liniment, and be certain that the bones from which it began will not be feared or their dissolution.
If the bone protrudes from the location and you remove the bandage, then you break it, take it out, and proceed as described in the treatment for a broken furculum or an abscess cauterized with care.

Chapter VIII.

The restoration is to fill the fracture using the senses as possible, until it is healed; then apply adhesive and tighten the broken bone with a steel rod if necessary.
If the rib fracture descends into compartments, this causes severe pain for the patient and requires puncturing, as with puncturing for pleurisy; then cover the bone with a dressing; and this causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, fever, and vomiting of blood; and this is difficult to heal.

Chapter 9.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck or the vertebrae of the back. If something happens to someone and you want to know if they will heal or not, consider this: if their hands feel softened, numb, and dead, and you cannot make them move or strengthen them by pulling on them or pricking them with a needle and do not feel pain in them, then you know that they are not healing, and in most cases it is fatal.
If they feel contraction and pain in those areas when pulled or pricked, then you know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the patient will heal with treatment.
If you see vertebrae in the neck similar to that and want to know if they will heal or not, consider their weight (size).
If they are already softened and you see something that happened in the muscles (surrounding tissues), and when you press on their back it produces wind or stool involuntarily, and sometimes is placed supine on their back and wants to urinate but cannot, then you know that it is fatal, and do not waste time with the treatment of such cases.
But if no such cause occurs, the prognosis is better and easier.
The treatment is to perform the evacuation of the abscess by applying a hot oil cautery or poultices with mustard seeds on the swollen vertebrae, and apply it for several days until the abscess is drained.
Afterwards, apply a comforting and soothing poultice and strengthen it with linen; and tell the patient to rest, have peace of mind, not sleep on the affected side until it heals.
If it is necessary to perform the removal of the affected bone or any known cause that has been separated from it, we must saw the bone and remove it; then adjust the two lips of the wound, using suture if it is large; and treat it with a soothing salve made from ointments until it heals.
But if the posterior part of the bone is fractured and it is at the end of the coccyx, we must insert our left hand into the anus and move the fractured bone with the right hand as possible by equalizing; then apply a poultice and splint to it, if necessary, and strengthen it.
If you feel fractured vertebrae in the spine, we must perform an opening on that area, remove them, and treat the wound with the prescribed treatment until it heals.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the anus.
The bones of the anus sometimes get broken, and if they break, their fracture is nothing more than a rupture in the extremities and are inserted into the length and slope toward the internal parts; and the patient feels pain at that location and the anus becomes numb when it is fractured.
The restoration of it (the patient) is that you extend your hand over the entrance which is above the fracture, its figure (shape).
If the fracture is in the extremity (limb) of it alone, then you press that [part] according to what appears suitable to you by means of splinting inside, which resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the long bone or that descends toward the joints below, then you make the patient lie on their stomach to prepare for the restoration of that fracture. And when you have splinted that [part], place plaster over it and apply pressure to the wooden or metal splint, and tighten by compression on the area where you do not fear perforation of the fracture or displacement of the splint.
Splint the concave area with something that fills it so that the splint takes the same shape, and tell the patient to sleep on their side or on their back.
But if an abscess cauterization occurs, you cease extending and restoring it until the abscess cauterization subsides, and place something over it as directed; then return to restoring it, and tighten it as appropriate.
If this happens in the bones that are set or shattered, you treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the restoration of the dislocated joint fracture.
The dislocated joint is that which is between the collarbone and the head of the shoulder.
If it is broken, the restoration of it (the dislocated joint) is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a long flat wooden splint of medium thickness according to this form:
And they are attached at both ends with ligaments, and it should be suspended from a high place.
And the patient follows over a seat; and the broken arm is thrown out over the splint to reach the end of it in the middle of the location where the curve of the splint is attached.
And suspend it for some serious reason or let the minister extend it towards the lower parts; and the doctor and his assistants hold the fracture with their hands until the fracture returns as appropriate.
The other way is that the patient lies on their back with their neck extended and holds their arm above the fracture with a band; and you command two assistants, one of whom holds something over the fracture with their hands; and the other holds the swelling below it; and each extends to their respective sides.
If you want the extension to be stronger, then tighten over the fracture with a band and over it with another band, and each of the two assistants extends to their respective sides.
And if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, you must stop halfway along the band over the joint and the other part of the band below the shoulder.
Similarly, if the fracture is near the collarbone, you must stop halfway along the band at that location and at the collarbone.
Splint the dislocated joint with ease and without trouble, and once it is splinted according to what is appropriate and applied well, tighten it if there is no abscess cauterization.
But if an abscess cauterization occurs, you should loosen the splint for up to seven days and place linen soaked in warm vinegar and rose oil over it until the abscess subsides, and then tighten it again.
The method of tightening the splint is to place plaster over the fracture and then cover it with a linen wrap; and adjust the arm above the dislocated joint, and place a cushion under the patient's muscle.
Place the linen and tighten over the dislocated joint the arm to keep it in place without any obstacles due to the broken bone.
Use splints (wooden or metal).
You should place the splint over the fracture that is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place a gap between each splint equal to the width of the finger, and the length of the splints according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on all sides.
Approach strongly upon the stars with the constriction that was mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And that your constriction on the location of the fracture be made stronger. And as much as the fracture has healed, the constriction should be reduced.
And thus, when the patient's condition of the stars and the constriction are as we said at the time you restore the member to its proper position, then you do this.
And if you fear an abscess, leave the constriction and stars until the seventh day as we said.
Then consider the salve on all parts for three days, so that it does not happen in the affected area pain or inflammation; or food is forbidden to reach the member due to excess of constriction; and thus correct everything as we said.
But if you are certain that no cause of this arises, do not unbind the ligature before three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands over their stomach.
Place a cushion full of wool soaked in vinegar over the broken limb, and consider at all times during the night or day that the figure of the broken member is not to be unbound or dissolved; or the ligament is softened, thus correct everything with your skill.
Restrict the patient's food according to the order we gave earlier, so that the food is subtle at first until it becomes stronger; and thus coarse up their food.
Since it is customary for the cushion and the mat of him to be strengthened for forty days; and thus they must be unbound and bathed, and treated with poultices that correct this.
And if the fracture is very deep, you do not unbind that ligament and the stars for up to two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is composed of two bones that are called the ulna and radius; one is small, which is referred to as the lower bone, and the other is large and is called the lower ulna, located below the lower bone.
If only the larger bone is broken or if both are broken.
And when the lower bone is broken above, the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the upper bone is broken below, the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
Worse yet if both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the lower bone is broken above, it is appropriate for the doctor to wait a small extension with ease before binding it.
But if the larger bone is broken, you must wait a more vigorous extension.
If both bones are broken, you must wait for a less forceful reduction.
You must bind the hand of the arm, restore it and extend it on a cushion, and place the thumb above, as we have said earlier, and the auricular is lower than the others, and the patient lies down on a chair with a backrest.
Place a cushion in front of him at his height or incline, so that the patient does not suffer labor.
And extend the arm from below with your hand or ligament, and another servant extends it above in the same way.
And bind the bone until it reduces as well as possible.
If in the fracture of the bone there are shards, you must remove everything from its location with your skill.
If a shard appears separated that penetrates the skin and is not in your hope for its restoration, you must perform surgery on it and remove it as we have said above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, we have already dealt with that in a separate chapter, so take care of it.
If an abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, you line up a cloth with a lining made of rose oil and white wax, medium thickness and strength, and apply strong wrapping over it with subtle constriction until the abscess subsides; then remove the lining and place a paste prepared from powdered crushed walnut shells and clay of huou, and cover the stars; and let the star that is placed above the fracture be larger and stronger.
Know that the number of stars in the arm is usually in the major positions, or the fracture in one bone or in two bones together.
And apply your constriction on the location of the fracture forcefully and vigorously, and when more extension is required above or below the fracture, reduce the constriction slightly as remembered in the beginning of the chapter.
The cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or harsh, and the thread with which it is strengthened should be of medium thickness between subtle and coarse as we have recommended.
Consider the member and ligament for several days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, such as a swelling occurring in a member, and then it becomes necessary to immerse the member in warm water until the swelling subsides.
Leave the member unrestrained until it returns to its normal state, and then restore the affected area.
And if the swelling has become soft and the bone has been restored to its natural state, then correct everything with your skill.
If the nutrition is blocked from reaching the member due to excessive swelling, then it becomes necessary to soften it slightly and allow it to rest for several days until the nutrition reaches it again, and strengthen it.
If the patient does not experience any of those mentioned causes, there is no need to keep him bedridden for more than approximately twenty days or so.
Suspend the hand of the patient using your skill to prevent it from moving too much, and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture is restored in thirty days or sixty days, or less in twenty-eight days, depending on the complications and the doctor's expertise.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a finger or thumb fracture.
Know that when the joint of the hand and the knuckles of the fingers break, it is not immediately apparent, but rather slow movement indicates it.

And if it returns quickly, that is good; otherwise, you should strengthen it with plaster and allow it to rest; do not apply any more ointment or heat, as it may cause pain or prevent the reduction of the dislocation for several days.
However, the joint may remain crooked; and do not inform the patient of any cause, but make their hand soft and not strong enough to exert any force; then know that the nerves are cut or crushed, and do not be hasty in healing it, but rather strengthen it with a pointed cautery for its recovery and confer no cause.
Therefore, when the shoulder is returned, you should place it on the plaster which has been recommended, and allow it to rest and strengthen for seven days; then release it, let it rest again, and exercise the hand of the patient.
And if the motion is impeded and there are any complications, then alleviate it with warm water and gentle friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Fingers are sometimes dislocated at various places.
When one finger is dislocated at the base of the palm or in the middle, then hold it and push the dislocation back into its position until it returns.
And support the finger's head, and suspend the affected area, allowing it to rest for two days.
Suspend the finger and stretch it until it is straightened on that day.
When night comes, follow that order, and do not cease to release it during the day and exercise it with motion, and lay it down at night; and do this for several days until it is straightened.
And do the same if it is dislocated in the wrist of the hand, and lay it down at that side and perform the first operation on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do as when it is dislocated in other places.
Here starts capital thirty.
On the treatment of dislocation of the spine bones in the back.
When any of the spine or neck bones are completely dislocated and more than two vertebrae are moved from their places, there is no cure for that, because death approaches the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's bowel movement is outside their control and they cannot retain it; and many times some limbs or both legs or one arm, or even one vertebra is moved from its place, because many times it is moved and many times it is made to move to four different directions.
Therefore, it has already been dislocated in the back and is called scoliosis.
But the treatment of it is whether it occurs in childhood, because in that case there is neither cure nor healing of everything.
But in that which occurs by accident or by impact or similar to those, the ancients have already spoken at length about the methods of treatment; but I will abbreviate it from that which has a little excuse for its excessive length, because I want to present and form it with different instruments than the one which I will explain and expand upon.
And only that one which occurs in the back itself with what I will recommend is cured.
And the patient should be extended flat on their back, facing a wall, and should be supported below to prevent pressure on their legs.
And a straight stick should be placed, fixed in the ground at the end near their head, and another stick in front of them to support their feet at the other end.
The attendant should hold the stick, not being anchored in a hole; and another attendant should hold the other in the same way.
And wrap around the head of the patient with a cloth and tighten it firmly with a bandage; extend the end of the bandage to the stick that is near their head, and tie it there; and strongly apply another bandage around their waist and hips, under their chest, and adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other stick which is under their feet.
Approach the minister and extend the linen to the division of the trunk, but do not allow the two trunks and the linens in their places where they are inserted, but rather loosen them slightly.
The doctor places his hands on the spine with strength until it returns, or places a board on top of it and adjusts his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return in this manner, then take a board whose length or width is three feet, and dig a hole in the wall next to the patient, into which you insert the end of the board. Then place the edge of the board on the spine and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and exert pressure until the spine is bent and returns to its location.
And if you wish, turn the patient's head to your hand; and you should place his heels at the end of two trunks, whose length is one forearm each, and his head similarly at the end of two other trunks, with a space between them that is a palm for each trunk. Then make a hole in each trunk and secure it firmly to the ground so that it does not move.
Make one trunk round and place it on top of the two trunks.
And at the end of it is a hole through which the trunk is formed, with a length of one palm and a circle around it. And the other two trunks are similar to this.
Then tighten the linens that you have stretched on the feet of the patient in the hole where the head is to be placed, and the linens that you have stretched on the thighs of the patient in the ring where the legs are to be placed.
Then each turn should have a servant who turns the ring with his hand, and the doctor should insert the spine as stated.
Once the spine is returned and the location is covered, it is necessary to place compresses made of thick plaster with albumen from eggs and stopper, and to place a board over the plaster, whose width is three fingers or more around that area, and the length is the same as the size of the swelling, and on top of the spine's location.
And use the linens that were mentioned earlier for nourishment until the patient is healed.
If there remains any danger in the location at the end of healing, it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe it for a long time with the administration of the board, which we have recommended; and already a layer of lead has been applied to that location.
Sometimes there is danger in the lower end of the spinal column due to dislocation; and it is cautioned for that reason that this medicine should not be applied quickly to it because it can lead to death.
Chapter .xxxi.
On the treatment of a dislocated pelvis.
Know that the joint of the pelvis and the joint of the muscle do not dislocate but only displace, and this does not happen to those joints that dislocate slightly and are concave.
And the joint of the pelvis is dislocated in four ways: it can be dislocated internally or externally, anteriorly or posteriorly.
And moreover, it is dislocated internally and rarely anteriorly or posteriorly.
When the dislocation of the joint at the hip is suspected, since you adjust the patient's healthy leg next to the affected one, it becomes longer and the affected leg is more prominent than the healthy one, and the affected leg cannot bend; therefore, it should be straightened, and it is the place where you should apply a cauterized cautery incision, so that the head of the dislocation has been reached here.
The sign of the dislocation which occurs at the hip is that the symptoms opposite to this accident occur there.
The sign of the dislocation at the front of the hip is when the leg becomes extended according to its nature, but does not bend due to pain that is on the inside, and if it is forced to be moved and cannot move inwardly; and the urine is retained and the incision is cauterized, and you should walk with it on the heel.
The sign of the dislocation at the back of the hip is that the inside does not expand, nor can it bend outward which bends the incision, and the leg becomes shorter than the other, and the incision becomes soft, and the head of the dislocation should be there.
But the reduction of these types of dislocations depends on your consideration, if the dislocation is old and longstanding, which has already been treated with approximation, then it is not in its cure; and therefore, there should be no expectation for improvement.
But in the case of a recent dislocation and one of the four types of dislocations, then you should aim to bring the joint together and extend it outwardly on both sides, with the right and left, for a strong pull returns and does not require any other treatment.
But if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare and apply strong treatment and extend the leg from the lower part or with the aid of a lever that is tied above the dislocation; and another assistant should extend the hand above it so that his fingers reach the end of it; and apply pressure firmly on the ligament above the incision, and have another assistant hold the end of the ligament tightly.
And the extension should be either from the inside of the leg to the incision at the groin, or from the back of the leg to the end of the spine.
And the extension of all these should be performed in one move while the patient is raised off the ground and remains hanging.
This type of extension is common to all four types.
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what has been said, it will be good; and if not, it is necessary for all types to follow what I will say about its own treatment.
In the reduction of its own, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is at the inside parts, it is necessary that the patient lies down on their back; and place the ligaments on the incision in the location between the head of the dislocation and the lower part where the incision is to be straightened; and extend the ligament above the area of the groin to the most advanced part of the spine.
And have another assistant hold the arms of the patient tightly, and grip the large head of the dislocated joint firmly and extend it outwardly by forceful extension, for it will return to its place.
And this type is easier cure than the other types with which this member is reduced.
But if it is excused and you do not obey this type of treatment for all, then it is necessary to tie the two feet of the patient together with a strong, light ligament around both ankles and both soles.
And the length of each should be four fingers, and the affected leg should be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And have the patient suspended over the post, with their head upward; and let the length of them from the ground be two arms.
And command a strong young man to press down on the head of the dislocation, and have another young man support the patient, and the other young man should press firmly on the joint with strong force, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the reduction of its own, when it is a dislocation on the outside parts, it is necessary for the patient to walk with crutches as recommended earlier; and tighten the ligament firmly on the affected leg and on the feet.
And pause two posts, one on the feet and the other on the head; and pause ropes attached to the middle of each post, which damp cloths should be wrapped around, so that the patient is not hurt because the post is between their legs, and so that the extension outwardly is not restricted.
And each assistant should extend to their respective part, and the doctor with his hand guides the reduction according to art.
And when dislocation occurs at the hips, it is necessary that the leg be gently extended, and according to this account on hip; and the doctor places the palm of his right hand on the affected hip, and with the other hand he presses that one, and with that pressure he holds the dislocated hip back towards the opposite hip.
And if dislocation occurs at the hips behind, it is not necessary that the patient be extended towards the back and raised above the ground; rather, it must be held in place on a hard surface, just as it should be held if the groin is dislocated to the outside of the hips, as we have said earlier about the treatment of such cases. And it must be on its face and the limbs extended, according to what we have said, there is no improvement.
It is necessary to apply a compression with a board to the joint area.
Therefore, as the reduction is complex, as the sign for reduction of that joint is not hidden from you (you spread the patient's legs) and when you see the groins contracted and the patient extends their leg and expands it without excuse, then you know which member needs to be returned - and then you adjust two cushions and pack it with bandages so that the groin is not damaged in any part; and you keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the bandages and the packing, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see that the groins are aligned properly, then you know that the dislocation is now closed, and at that point you can let the patient move about.
If you see any sign of swelling, then return and pack it, and extend it according to your first structure, and let it rest for three days.
Then unbind that one, and make him walk slowly on crutches for several days until he is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
On the treatment of hip dislocation.
The hip can be dislocated in three ways: to the outside, to the inside, more towards the back than the front; but it is not dislocated to the front.
And the sign for hip dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply pressure to their thigh, and then you know that the hip is dislocated.
And the treatment for all types of dislocation is to position the patient correctly, so that they extend their leg if possible, place your hands on the side of it, and hold the foot between your feet; and bring the hip towards you with both hands and hold it in place firmly, and another person extend the foot until the hip returns to its proper position.
The sign for reduction of dislocation is that the leg adheres to the thigh without laborious effort.
Then pack that one and make the leg adhere to the thigh with bandages for three or four days, and then unbind it.
Do not allow the patient to walk for several days until they are healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong traction with the bandages as described in the treatment of the groin that was mentioned earlier.
Chapter XXXIII.
On the treatment of ankle dislocation.
The ankle is sometimes reduced by slight movement, and sometimes it is dislocated due to complications, and the dislocation of it is made either towards the inside or outside.
And the sign for dislocation of the ankle is that you see the ankle swollen in the direction towards which it has been dislocated.
But the treatment for reduction is gentle, and it should be extended easily with both hands and held in place until it returns to its proper position.
But in the treatment of ankle dislocation due to complications, it is necessary that the patient be positioned correctly, and have a strong and capable person from behind the foot on the same side of the dislocation.
And place your right hand on the upper part of the foot and your left hand on the lower part of the foot in the area of the calf.
And pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and pull it again with your left hand for two steps, and extend it with your left hand; and push the toes of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as they have counted.
Then you push the feet of the patient to the bed on the third observation, and you are at the foot-end.
If it returns in one observation or two according to this method, and if the foot appears swollen, that is good; but if not, you continue with the operation, because it will return.
But if the reduction of it is forbidden as they have recommended, then you place the patient on his back on the ground, spread a cloth on the ground and let him lie between his legs, and you are already wrapped around him with a cloth so that you do not hurt the patient.
Then the one in charge of him should hold his legs, and another minister should stretch the foot with his hands or as he lies on the neck of the foot; and each minister should extend their arms opposite to the extension of the patient's leg, and the cloth should be placed between the patient's legs to keep the body from being pulled apart at the sides, so that its extension is not torn.
And the doctor grasps the dislocation with both hands, and another minister holds the healthy limb apart, because the dislocation returns easily.
And when the dislocation returns and the reduction of it is declared to you as good, then you stop plastering and tightly bind the foot on the bed.
And be careful or protect yourself from the nerves that are behind the heel, because the binding should not be violent and hurt it strongly.
And leave it for two days or three; and if the binding is softened, remove it.
Then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he must go before that space, it is not certain that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted, and this will not receive treatment.
If an abscess is formed by cautery, then you must administer the prescribed treatment for it as in the place of care and binding mentioned earlier, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the cure of dislocation of the fingers of the feet.
It is necessary that the one who is dislocated be reduced with a little extension without strain; and this is not difficult, it is easy to move.
But if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, it is necessary that you place the patient on a stool or table at the site for reduction and be standing as if he were walking.
And you stand on one foot, and place your other foot on the site of the joints, and press it with the other foot with force until it returns, and observe that it has been reduced and does not appear in the prominent area.
And place the foot of it on a table that supports the entire foot, which has two legs; and tighten it with a suitable and firm structure for three days; and then unbind it and let it rest from walking for several days, until it is strengthened and the reduction is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
On the kinds of dislocation that are caused by a wound, or a fracture, or any combination thereof.
When some cause of that kind occurs and you devote yourself to the treatment of it and the restoration of the limb, many observations may occur before death.
And therefore, in the treatment of it, do not be presumptuous or hasty, but rather patient, humble, and compassionate; use remedies that allow abscesses to heal slowly; leave the patient to divine judgment, but treat him who you hope will recover from danger, with alleviation of the disease, and in which you see some hope. And therefore, as soon as possible, make the reduction at the beginning of the cause before an abscess forms.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer treatment that allows an abscess to form and cures the wound with appropriate drying remedies.
And if the dislocation is caused by a fracture and it occurs in separated bones, then you perform a setting of them, and do what has been said earlier about simple diseases at their sites.
And guard and avoid, according to your ability, the intestine in fearful paths as my preaching has guided you; for that is better for you, and it preserves your creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more healthy to your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Albuscasim, may God be praised. Amen.
